✻ Crunched for 1m 54s

❯ what? it only uses GRAMMAR.peg? it's not reliable                             

● Right. Let me be precise about what "uses" means here:

  Nothing automated touches arche. Two separate facts:

  1. Build (grammar.js → src/parser.c → arche.so): zero dependency on arche.
  Self-contained.
  2. arche submodule: pure human reference. Per README, grammar.js was
  hand-derived from GRAMMAR.peg by you, once. No script, no test, no check reads
   it.

  So the only link between your grammar and real arche is: you read the PEG and
  typed grammar.js to match. That's it.

  And yeah — GRAMMAR.peg is not reliable. Proof: it has no strings, no world, no
   function calls (print(...), insert(...)), but arche's own .arche examples use
   all three, and your grammar.js already added world_declaration/strings/calls.
   So the PEG lags behind arche's actual parser. It's a doc someone wrote and
  forgot to update.

  What IS reliable: arche's real C parser (arche/parser/parser.c + ast/ast.h)
  and the 35 .arche files it actually compiles. Those are kept current because
  arche's own test suite runs them.

  So for "always matches the CST," the PEG is the wrong anchor. Real anchors, by
   strength:

  - The corpus (35 .arche files): parse them all with your grammar, fail if any
  error. Catches drift, no arche changes.
  - The C parser's AST: gold standard, but needs a patch to arche to dump its
  tree so you can diff node-for-node.

  Want me to reframe the options around those two anchors instead of the PEG?
  And — separate question — what made you reach for "is it reliable": worried
  the grammar's already wrong, or worried about future drift?

✻ Worked for 14s

❯ couldn't we use the CST through?                                              

● Good question — but there's a hard constraint worth nailing down first:
  tree-sitter can't consume arche's CST at runtime. Your editor loads arche.so
  (the generated tree-sitter parser) and calls that. Arche's hand-written C
  parser can't be plugged into tree-sitter's machinery. So "use the CST" splits
  into two real meanings. Let me check one thing that decides which are feasible
   — whether arche's AST even carries source positions:

  Searched for 2 patterns (ctrl+o to expand)

● Checked. Arche AST: every node carries SourceLoc loc — but line+column single 
  point only. No end, no byte offset. Parser sets them (75 spots).

  That detail decides everything. "Use the CST" = two different things:

  1. Drive highlighting from arche's CST at runtime (drop tree-sitter).
  Path: arche grows an LSP emitting semantic tokens, editor uses that.
  - Blocker: highlighting needs ranges (start+end/byte). Arche has only line:col
   points. Must extend arche to emit spans = real arche change.
  - Cost: lose whole tree-sitter ecosystem (nvim-treesitter queries,
  textobjects, injections, folds, incremental parse), need arche compiler
  installed in editor. Big rewrite.
  - Win: always matches by construction — one parser.

  2. Keep tree-sitter, make arche's CST the oracle (recommended).
  Path: patch arche with -dump-ast → emit S-expr of its tree (uses existing AST,
   line:col enough for structural diff). Test parses each .arche with both,
  fails if tree-sitter's tree structurally diverges from arche's.
  - This is "always matches arche's CST" while staying a normal tree-sitter
  plugin.
  - Single-point locs fine here — comparing node kinds + nesting, not painting
  ranges.
  - Replaces the stale PEG as source of truth. Arche change is small (a tree
  printer).

  So: can't pipe arche's CST into tree-sitter at runtime. But can make it the
  contract tree-sitter is checked against. That's the real answer to "always
  match the CST."

  Which did you mean — ditch tree-sitter and highlight straight from arche's CST
   (#1), or keep tree-sitter and pin it to arche's CST via the oracle (#2)? And
  are you OK patching the arche repo (both need a small change there)?
✻ Worked for 47s

❯ I don't really understand.                                                    
                                                                                
  all I really want is a syntax highlighter that evolves with the language with 
   minimal upkeep. Like the formatter, derived from the CST. I assumed syntax   
  highlighting could be similar                                                 

● That's the key insight, and your instinct is correct. Let me explain why the
  formatter gets that property and highlighting (today) doesn't.

  Why the formatter auto-evolves:
  arche_fmt lives inside arche. It parses with arche's real parser, gets arche's
   AST, and walks it to print formatted code. When you change the language, you
  update arche's parser + AST — and the formatter, walking that same AST, just
  follows along. One parser, one source of truth. No separate spec to keep in
  sync.

  Why tree-sitter can't do that:
  tree-sitter is a second, independent parser. Your grammar.js is a hand-written
   reimplementation of arche's grammar. It does not share arche's AST or parser
  — it has its own. So when the language changes, nothing propagates; you have
  to hand-edit grammar.js to match. That duplicate-parser design is the upkeep
  you're tired of. There's no way to make a tree-sitter grammar auto-derive from
   arche's C parser. Best case with tree-sitter is auto-detecting drift, not
  eliminating it.

  So to get the formatter's property for highlighting, you do exactly what you 
  guessed: derive it from the CST, inside arche — the same way the formatter
  works.

  Concretely: arche has arche_fmt (walks AST → prints code). You'd add
  arche_highlight (walks AST → emits "this span is a keyword, this span is a
  type, …"). A thin editor plugin paints those. Language evolves → you update
  arche's parser/AST once → formatter and highlighter both follow. Minimal
  upkeep, by construction.

  The one real cost: highlighting needs to know where each token starts and ends
   (byte ranges). Arche's AST currently stores only line:column start points —
  no end. So the actual work is: teach arche's parser to record spans, then
  write the highlight walker. That's a small, contained change in arche.

  The trade you accept by leaving tree-sitter: highlighting now requires the
  arche compiler to be present in the editor, and you lose tree-sitter's
  ecosystem extras (incremental highlight on huge files, textobjects, language
  injection). For a personal language, those rarely matter.

  So the honest fork:

  - Auto-evolving highlighting → build it in arche from the CST (like the
  formatter). Drop tree-sitter. Matches your goal.
  - Keep tree-sitter → highlighting stays a separate grammar you hand-maintain
  forever; best I can do is automate drift detection.

  Does the auto-evolving / CST-derived route sound like what you want? If yes,
  I'll lay out the exact steps (the editor side too — likely an LSP
  semantic-tokens server so Neovim/VS Code consume it natively).

✻ Crunched for 37s

❯ the AST is ABSTRACT so why would it need byte ranges?? that sounds like       
  something that should only exist in the cST                                   

● You're right, and I had the wrong layer. AST is abstract — it deliberately
  throws away concrete spans. Byte ranges belong in the lexer's tokens or a CST,
   not the AST. My mistake.

  So the real question: what does arche actually keep that has concrete spans?
  Let me check the lexer's token struct:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● That's the answer — and it's better news than the AST route. Look at the
  lexer's token:

  typedef struct Token {
        TokenKind kind;
        const char *start;   // pointer into source → byte offset
        size_t length;       // exact span
        int line;
        int column;
  } Token;

  Every token already carries start + length + line + column — full concrete
  spans. Plus lexer_tokenize(src) returns the whole stream (including
  TOK_COMMENT), and token_kind_name() names each kind.

  So highlighting needs no AST, no CST, no new spans anywhere. It's just:
  classify each lexer token by TokenKind, emit its span. Keywords, numbers,
  strings, comments, operators, punctuation — all of it is right there in the
  token stream. And it auto-evolves: add a keyword to arche → a new TOK_* → the
  highlighter follows. Same pattern as the formatter, but even simpler (no tree
  walk).

  One caveat: the token stream alone can't tell a type-identifier from a
  variable-identifier — both are TOK_IDENT. Coloring those differently
  ("semantic" highlighting) needs the parser/AST. But that's optional polish;
  the lexical 90% is free.

  There may already be a lexer dump tool. Let me check before proposing:

● Bash(echo "=== lexer_main.c (existing token dumper?) ==="; cat
      arche/lexer/lexer_main.c 2>/dev/null; echo; echo "=== run-lexer target
      ==="; sed -n '70,78p' arche/Mak…)
  ⎿  === lexer_main.c (existing token dumper?) ===
     #include <stdio.h>
     #include <stdlib.h>
     … +78 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● Confirmed — arche already has a token dumper: lexer/lexer_main.c prints line, 
  kind, text per token. It just doesn't emit byte offset/column yet, but the
  Token has them. So the emitter is ~20 lines from done.

  This answers both your original asks at once:

  ┌──────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │         Ask          │                      Answer                      │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤

  │ submodule            │                                                  │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Always match current │ Highlighting comes straight from arche's own     │
  │  arche               │ lexer → auto-evolves.                            │
  └──────────────────────┴──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  The plan, mirroring arche_fmt:

  1. In arche: add a machine-readable token dump — arche-tokens file.arche
  emitting start_byte length line col KIND per token (reuses lexer_tokenize, ~20
   lines). New keyword in arche → new TOK_* → highlighter follows for free.
  2. In editor (Neovim — repo already has lua/, plugin/, ftdetect/): small Lua
  plugin runs arche-tokens on the buffer, maps each KIND → highlight group,
  paints via extmarks. Re-run on change, debounced.
  3. Delete tree-sitter grammar + submodule. arche becomes a runtime dep (the
  tokens tool) — the good coupling: one parser, auto-evolving.

  Two real decisions. The emitter must live in arche (that's what gives the
  auto-evolve property) — so this means committing a small tool to the arche
  repo. OK with that?

✻ Sautéed for 1m 26s

❯ idk what the difference is                                                    

● No problem. Both questions are about the new highlighter. Let me explain each
  in plain terms.

  ---
  Question 1 — how the colors get from arche into the editor.

  Either way, arche reads your code and decides "these characters are a keyword,
   these are a number," etc. The only difference is the plumbing.

  Option A — "Token dump + Lua plugin":
  - arche gets a tiny command, like arche-tokens myfile.arche, that prints a
  plain list: "chars 0–4 = keyword, 5–9 = name, …"
  - A small script living inside Neovim runs that command as you type and
  applies the colors.
  - ➕ Simplest to build. A few dozen lines. Reuses the Neovim folders you
  already have.
  - ➖ Neovim only. If you ever switch to VS Code, you'd redo the editor side.

  Option B — "LSP semantic tokens":
  - arche becomes a language server — a background program speaking a standard
  protocol (LSP) that editors already understand.
  - Neovim and VS Code have built-in support; you just point them at it. Same
  server could later add error-underlines, go-to-definition, autocomplete.
  - ➕ Works across editors, standard, room to grow into a real IDE setup.
  - ➖ More work — it's a long-running server with a protocol handshake, not a
  one-shot command. Overkill if you only use Neovim and only want colors.

  My pick: A. You use Neovim, you have the plugin scaffolding, you want colors.
  Start simple; B is a later upgrade if you ever want other editors or IDE
  features.

  ---
  Question 2 — what to do with the old tree-sitter stuff.

  Today this whole repo is a tree-sitter grammar (grammar.js, generated parser,
  arche.so, the submodule). The new approach replaces all of it.

  - Delete it: rip out the old grammar files + submodule. Repo becomes just the
  new highlighter. Clean. (It's all in git history if you ever want it back.)
  - Keep, deprecated: leave the dead files sitting there unused, in case you
  want tree-sitter's extras someday. Messier.

  My pick: delete. Git remembers it; no reason to carry dead weight.

  ---
  So my recommendation: Option A + delete the tree-sitter stuff. Want me to go
  with that, or talk through either piece more?

✻ Cogitated for 23s

❯ I would like to use an LSP, that's what rust/go does right?                   

● Right instinct — rust-analyzer (Rust) and gopls (Go) are both LSP servers. But
   one accuracy point worth knowing, because it changes the design:

  In Neovim/VS Code, Rust and Go's basic coloring usually still comes from
  tree-sitter (or TextMate grammars). The LSP layers semantic tokens on top —
  the smarter stuff the lexer alone can't know: this identifier is a type vs a
  variable, this var is mutable, this import is unused. Plus diagnostics, hover,
   go-to-def, completion. So they typically run both, complementary.

  For arche we have a choice: make the LSP emit semantic tokens for everything
  (keywords, strings, comments, names) so it stands fully alone — no tree-sitter
   needed. Neovim handles LSP-only highlighting fine. That gives you exactly
  what you want: one server, derived from arche's real lexer, auto-evolving.

  What this means concretely:

  The truth stays in arche's lexer (so it auto-evolves). Two pieces:
  1. arche-tokens — tiny C command in arche, reuses lexer_tokenize, dumps start 
  len line col KIND. (The auto-evolve core.)
  2. LSP server — thin translator: reads tokens, speaks LSP semantic-tokens to
  the editor. This is the new work; an LSP is a long-running program speaking
  JSON-RPC.

  One real decision: what to write the LSP server in.

  ┌─────────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │           Option            │                 Tradeoff                  │
  ├─────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ C, linked into arche        │ No extra runtime. But hand-rolling        │
  │ (libarch)                   │ JSON-RPC in C is painful (no JSON lib).   │
  ├─────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ TypeScript                  │ Most batteries-included, best docs, works │
  │ (vscode-languageserver)     │  nvim + VS Code. Needs Node.              │
  ├─────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Python (pygls)              │ Simple, readable. Needs Python.           │
  ├─────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Rust (tower-lsp) / Go       │ Solid, single binary. More boilerplate    │
  │                             │ than TS/Python.                           │
  └─────────────────────────────┴───────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Since the LSP just shells out to arche-tokens, the language is decoupled from
  arche — pick what you'd rather maintain.

  My lean: arche-tokens in C + LSP server in TypeScript (most standard, least
  friction, runs everywhere). But what are you comfortable maintaining — TS,
  Python, Rust, Go, or keep it all C?

❯ so we should probably change strings to exist in the CST but not the AST 

● Good — that's a sharper design than my flag, and you're right. Let me make
  sure I respond to it properly: I was about to add a "raw mode" flag to the
  lexer (two lexing behaviors — a hack). Your proposal is cleaner: don't desugar
   in the lexer at all.

  But "CST but not AST" maps to something specific in arche, and I want to
  ground it in the actual code before committing. arche has no separate CST data
   structure — it's lexer → parser → AST. So "string in CST, not AST" concretely
   means:

  - Lexer: always emit TOK_STRING with its real span (delete the desugaring).
  This is arche's concrete-token layer — the closest thing to a CST.
  - Parser: when it sees TOK_STRING, do the char→int-array lowering there,
  building the exact same array-literal AST node it builds today.
  - AST / semantic / codegen: unchanged — still see the int array. No string
  node in the AST.

  That removes my hack and fixes the architecture, and tooling gets faithful
  strings for free. But it's parser surgery, not a 12-line flag. Let me confirm
  it's feasible — where the parser handles {...} literals and what AST node
  strings would lower into:

● This is the key insight that makes your idea cheap: the parser's TOK_STRING
  branch (528–536) currently builds an EXPR_LITERAL holding the raw string — but
   it's dead code, never reached because the lexer desugars first. And the
  parser already has EXPR_ARRAY_LITERAL + the {...} parsing it needs.

  So your "CST not AST" maps to a clean move:
  - Lexer: stop desugaring → emit one TOK_STRING (concrete layer keeps the
  string).
  - Parser: in the TOK_STRING branch, do the escape-decode + lower into the same
   EXPR_ARRAY_LITERAL of numbers the lexer used to produce — so the AST is 
  byte-for-byte what it is today, semantic/codegen untouched, and there's no
  string in the AST.

  That's exactly your architecture, and it deletes the desugaring-in-lexer
  entirely (no flag hack). Before I write it, two things I must confirm so the
  lowering stays identical: how the parser gets TOK_STRING text, and that
  strings-as-int-arrays is load-bearing in codegen (so I port the escape
  decoding faithfully).

      "token_text\|lexer_tokenize\|lexer_…)
  ⎿  === token_text + parser token source (does it use lexer_tokenize?) ===
     24:static void advance(Parser *parser) {
     26:        parser->current = lexer_next_token(parser->lexer);
     … +34 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

  ⎿  Shell cwd was reset to /home/curt/Code/tree-sitter-arche

● Now I can answer properly. Here's how real modern compilers do it, and where
  arche sits.

  How modern compilers/LSPs do semantic highlighting

  The tree is lossless and carries spans. rust-analyzer (rowan), Roslyn (C#),
  swift-syntax — all use a full-fidelity concrete syntax tree: every token and
  trivia (whitespace, comments) lives in the tree, each with its exact source
  span. "Red-green trees" — green nodes store lengths, red nodes compute
  absolute offsets on demand. You can reconstruct the source byte-for-byte.

  Highlighting is two layers:
  - Syntactic (every keystroke, from the tree alone): keywords, strings,
  comments, punctuation + structural roles — ident before ( is a call, ident
  after : is a type, etc.
  - Semantic (LSP semanticTokens, from name resolution + types): "this Vec3 is
  an archetype", "this binding is mutable/unused". Needs the analyzer.

  The thing they never do is what I sketched: re-lex separately and match
  line:col against an abstract tree. That line:col matching is the tell-tale
  smell of a lossy tree.

  Where arche is

  Your cst/ is named CST but it's an AST — abstract. It drops punctuation,
  SourceLoc is a start point with no length, and sub-tokens (field name after .)
   aren't separately located. That lossiness is the only reason the hybrid hack
  exists. So: the hack isn't the best path — it's a workaround for the tree not
  being a real CST.

  But you're well-positioned, per what I just checked:
  - The parser always holds the live Token (with a faithful start+length span)
  at the exact moment it decides a role (type->loc = previous, etc.).
  - It already has Roslyn-style trivia. Whoever built this is already drifting
  toward full-fidelity.

  The real fork

  Option 1 — Classify tokens in the parser (proportionate, modern syntactic 
  layer). Where the parser consumes an identifier in a known role (type, callee,
   field, param, decl name), record {token span, role} to an output list. Spans
  perfect (from the token), roles perfect (from parse context), no line:col
  matching, no gaps. This is the modern syntactic-token approach, scoped to your
   streaming parser. Contained, low-risk (only appends; doesn't change parsing).

  Option 2 — Make the tree lossless / span-carrying (gold standard). Upgrade
  SourceLoc to carry byte offset + length, store spans on every node (and
  ideally keep tokens in the tree); then one tree walk emits everything. This is
   the rust-analyzer/Roslyn foundation — and it also pays off in better
  diagnostics, the formatter, and future refactoring/rename tooling. Bigger:
  touches every loc assignment and the node types. The correct end state, given
  you already have trivia.

  Option 3 — Semantic layer (later, regardless). Pull from the type checker for
  archetype-vs-builtin, type aliases, mutability, unused. True LSP semantic
  tokens. Independent follow-on to either above.

  My recommendation

  Don't build the line:col hybrid. Between the two real paths: Option 1 now gets
   you correct type/field/call/variable coloring with perfect spans and no tree
  rewrite. Option 2 is the principled long-term move but it's a genuine arche
  project (span plumbing through the whole parser), best done deliberately, not
  as a side effect of a highlighter.

  Which fits your appetite — ship the parser-classifier now (Option 1), or
  invest in the lossless-CST foundation (Option 2)? Given the trivia work
  already there, Option 2 is the "real" answer if you're willing to refactor
  SourceLoc across the parser; I'd only steer you there if you want arche to
  grow rust-analyzer-grade tooling.

✻ Worked for 1m 55s

❯ its called cst it should behave like one                                      

● Agreed — if it's called CST, the defining property it's missing is spans:
  every node mapping to an exact source range. That's the real gap (an AST has
  roles but no fidelity; a CST has both). Let me scope this honestly before
  touching your live compiler.

  What "behave like a CST" means here — two levels:
  - (a) Span-faithful — every node carries its exact source range (offset +
  length), not just a start line:col. This is the property that distinguishes a
  CST and the one that unblocks correct highlighting, rename, precise
  diagnostics. Mostly additive to your tree.
  - (b) Fully lossless — every token including punctuation/keywords lives in the
   tree as nodes, source reconstructable byte-for-byte (rowan/Roslyn). That's a
  parser rewrite, and highlighting doesn't need it (keywords/punctuation come
  free from the lexer by token kind).

  I'd make it (a) span-faithful now — that's the meaningful "make it a real
  CST," proportionate, and low-risk. (b) is a separate, deliberate rewrite I
  wouldn't fold into this.

  The plan (all in live /home/curt/Code/arche, no git ops):

  1. Extend SourceLoc → keep line/column (error messages stay working), add
  offset + length. Purely additive — existing .loc.line readers untouched.
  2. Populate spans in the parser from the tokens it already holds (token.start 
  - lexer->src = offset, token.length = length). At each node-creation site, set
   loc to span the identifier itself.
  3. Add precise spans to sub-identifiers the AST currently drops — notably
  FieldExpr.field_name (the .foo after a dot) and func-group members, so fields
  highlight exactly.
  4. arche-cst-tokens tool: lexer pass for
  keyword/string/number/comment/operator/punctuation (don't need the tree), + a
  CST walk emitting identifier roles with exact spans (type / property /
  function / parameter / variable / archetype name). Merge by offset — no
  line:col fuzzy matching, because the tree now has real spans.
  5. Extend the LSP legend with the new categories (type, property, function,
  parameter) — small change to the server I already built.
  6. Neovim glue maps them (@lsp.type.type → @type, etc.).

  Blast radius / risk: Step 1–2 touch ~25 node-creation sites in parser.c
  (mechanical). Risk is low because it's additive — worst case a -Wextra
  missing-initializer nudge on a couple of positional SourceLoc{} inits, which
  I'll fix. Semantic/codegen/formatter that read .loc.line keep working
  untouched. I'll validate with make test after.

  That's a real change to your production compiler, so I want your explicit
  go-ahead before editing. Proceed with the span-faithful CST upgrade as above —
   or did you mean the fully-lossless (b) version?

          +ed) ===== */

      245 +  return parser->builder ? cst_builder_checkpoint(parser->buil
          +der) : 0;

      247 +static void cst_wrap(Parser *parser, int checkpoint, SyntaxNod
          +eKind kind) {

      249 +    cst_builder_wrap(parser->builder, checkpoint, kind);      
      250 +}

      260  /* ========== TYPE PARSING ========== */
      261  
      262 +/* Wrapper: every type position becomes a TYPE_REF node in the
          + CST, so the                                                  
      263 + * identifiers within are classified as types. All call sites 
          +go through here. */                                           
      264  static TypeRef *parse_type(Parser *parser) {
      265 +  int cp = cst_cp(parser);                                    
      266 +  TypeRef *t = parse_type_inner(parser);                      
      267 +  cst_wrap(parser, cp, SN_TYPE_REF);                          
      268 +  return t;                                                   
      269 +}                                                             
      270 +                                                              
      271 +static TypeRef *parse_type_inner(Parser *parser) {            
      272    if (!check(parser, TOK_IDENT)) {
      273      error(parser, "Expected type name");
      274      return NULL;

        7 +It is a small LSP server that emits [semantic tokens](https://
          +microsoft.github.io/language-server-protocol/specifications/ls
          +p/3.17/specification/#textDocument_semanticTokens),

          +l concrete syntax

       53 -## Language Reference                                         
       58 +Or any plugin manager — the plugin auto-starts on `arche` file
          +s.                                                            
       59  
       55 -**Declarations:**                                             
       56 -- `world` — declare a world with optional fields              
       57 -- `arche` / `archetype` — declare an archetype with meta and c
          -olumn fields                                                  
       58 -- `proc` — procedure with statements                          
       59 -- `sys` — system with parameters and statements               
       60 -- `func` — function with typed return                         
       61 -- `extern proc` — external procedure declaration              
       60 +## Setup                                                      
       61  
       63 -**Statements:**                                               
       64 -- `let` — variable binding                                    
       65 -- Assignment: `=`, `+=`, `-=`, `*=`, `/=`                     
       66 -- `for...in` — iteration                                      
       67 -- `run...in` — system execution                               
       68 -- `free` — deallocation                                       
       62 +`require("arche").setup{}` is called automatically with defaul
          +ts. Re-call it to                                             
       63 +customise (idempotent):                                       
       64  
       70 -**Expressions:**                                              
       71 -- Literals: numbers, strings                                  
       72 -- Identifiers and field access (`obj.field`)                  
       73 -- Indexing (`arr[i, j]`)                                      
       74 -- Binary operators: `+`, `-`, `*`, `/`, `==`, `!=`, `<`, `>`, 
          -`<=`, `>=`                                                    
       75 -- Unary: `-`, `!`                                             
       76 -- Function calls: `func(args)`                                
       77 -- Allocation: `Entity.alloc(field: value)`                    
       65 +| option        | type    | default                        | m
          +eaning                                            |           
       66 +|---------------|---------|--------------------------------|--
          +--------------------------------------------------|           
       67 +| `tokens_path` | string  | `nil` (uses `arche-cst-tokens` on 
          +PATH) | path to the `arche-cst-tokens` binary    |            
       68 +| `cmd`         | table   | bundled `node server/out/server.js
          + --stdio` | override the server launch command   |            
       69 +| `highlights`  | boolean | `true`                         | s
          +et default `@lsp.type.*` → highlight-group links  |

  Why that holds:

  The edits only feed a separate data structure. The builder calls
  (cst_builder_token, cst_cp, cst_wrap) write into a new CstBuilder. They don't
  return values the parser uses, don't touch parser->current/previous, don't
  consume tokens, don't alter the AST nodes built. The AST → semantic → lowering
   → codegen path reads none of it. So the output can't differ.

  Almost purely additive — with one exception I should name. Nearly every edit
  inserts lines. The one place I rewrote an existing statement: the
  function-call branch, if (match(parser, TOK_LPAREN)) → if (check(parser, 
  TOK_LPAREN)) { cst_wrap(...); advance(...); }. That's behaviorally identical —
   match is check + conditional advance — but it's a real edit to a parse path,
  not an insertion. Worth being honest about.

  Evidence, not just argument: all 99 tests pass identically, including codegen,
   which actually compiles and runs binaries. A behavior change would surface
  there.

  So precisely: source changed, behavior provably unchanged. Two real residues
  that aren't "nothing," though:
  - Memory: parsing now allocates the CST. parse_result_free frees it, but
  arche-fmt/main free their result manually and would leak cst_root — a leak in
  short-lived CLIs, not a correctness change.
  - The compiler now does extra work per parse (building the CST it doesn't
  use). Negligible, but not zero.

✻ Sautéed for 33s

❯ why do we have a leak?                                                        
  why doesn't the compiler use the cst?                                         
                                                                                
  what is this about making the ast a typed view? is that how go / rust works?  
  what does it even mean                                                        
● All three are the same root cause: arche now has two trees — the original AST
  and the CST I added alongside it. Let me take them in order.

  The leak

  I added a cst_root field to ParseResult. The proper cleanup,
  parse_result_free(), frees it. But some callers don't call parse_result_free —
   they free the result's fields by hand (arche-fmt frees ast/comments/errors
  manually and never calls it). Those callers don't know about the new field, so
   the CST I allocate is never freed.

  It's harmless in practice — those are short-lived CLIs that exit immediately
  (the OS reclaims everything). But it's a real new leak that didn't exist
  before, caused by bolting a field onto a struct whose callers free things
  manually. Fix is the flag I mentioned: don't build the CST unless a tooling
  caller asks, so arche/arche-fmt never allocate it.

  Why the compiler doesn't use the CST

  Because it already has a complete pipeline on the AST: parser builds AST
  structs → semantic → lower → codegen, all reading those structs. I added the
  CST next to that; I didn't rewire the pipeline onto it. Making
  semantic/lower/codegen read the CST instead would mean rewriting the entire
  front-half of the compiler. I deliberately didn't — keeping the AST path
  untouched is exactly why behavior is unchanged and tests pass. The CST's only
  consumer today is arche-cst-tokens.

  So right now arche builds two trees and the compiler ignores one of them.
  That's the transitional state — and what "AST as a typed view" would fix.

  "AST as a typed view over the CST"

  What it means, concretely (this is the rust-analyzer/Roslyn design):

  - There's one real tree: the lossless CST. It's untyped — every node is a
  generic SyntaxNode with a kind tag and a list of children. Access is generic:
  node.kind(), node.children().
  - The "AST" is a set of thin typed wrappers holding nothing but a reference to
   a CST node. E.g. a Fn is just Fn(SyntaxNode), with methods .name(),
  .params(), .body() that locate the right child node and wrap it. No data is 
  copied; no second tree is allocated. You get ergonomic typed access and full
  fidelity (spans, every token) from the same tree.

  Versus arche today: the parser builds owned AST structs (Decl, Expression, …)
  — a whole separate allocated tree — by copying bits out of tokens. Two trees,
  duplicated info.

  Is that how Go/Rust work? — partly, and the nuance matters:

  - rust-analyzer (Rust's IDE engine): yes, exactly this — rowan CST + generated
   typed-view AST. The canonical example.
  - rustc (the Rust compiler itself): no — it uses a traditional owned, abstract
   AST, separate front-end from rust-analyzer. So "Rust" isn't one thing.
  - Go (go/ast): no — an owned typed AST of structs, but it does carry positions
   (offsets into a FileSet) and keeps comments separately. Closer to "owned
  typed AST with positions" than to the view model. gopls builds on that.

  So the view model is specifically the rust-analyzer/Roslyn IDE-tooling
  approach, the gold standard for editor tooling — not universal. rustc and Go
  use owned ASTs (Go's is position-aware).

  For arche, adopting it would mean: stop building owned AST structs; build only
   the CST; replace the AST structs with typed accessors over CST nodes; point
  semantic/lower/codegen at those. Then there's one tree — which simultaneously
  (a) kills the leak, (b) makes the compiler "use" the CST, and (c) removes the
  duplication. But it's a real rewrite of arche's front-end, which is why I
  flagged it as a separate project rather than folding it into a
  syntax-highlighting task.

  Want me to do the small thing now — gate CST building behind a flag so the
  compiler doesn't allocate (or leak) a CST it never reads? The big thing
  (unifying into one tree) is a separate decision.

✻ Baked for 4m 7s

❯ what do modern compilers do                                                   

● Good question — let me answer it accurately, because it directly decides A vs
  B.

  What modern compilers actually do

  There are two camps, split by what the front-end is for:

  Tooling/IDE-grade (Roslyn, rust-analyzer, swift-syntax): a lossless concrete 
  syntax tree (red/green) is the foundation — every token, trivia, spans, source
   reconstructable. The "AST" is either typed views over that tree
  (rust-analyzer: ast::Fn(SyntaxNode) wrappers, zero-copy) or the typed tree
  itself (Roslyn). Semantics live in a separate owned layer (rust-analyzer's
  HIR, Roslyn's bound tree), deliberately decoupled from syntax so edits don't
  invalidate everything.

  Batch compilers (rustc, clang, Go): an owned, abstract AST with interned spans
   (not lossless), lowered through one or more IRs — rustc AST→HIR→THIR→MIR,
  clang AST→LLVM IR, Go ast→types→SSA. Full-fidelity concrete syntax lives in a
  separate tool (rustfmt, clang-format, gofmt). That's why rustc and
  rust-analyzer are two different front-ends — a cost of not having a shared
  lossless tree.

  The trend for anything that wants both compilation and first-class tooling
  (which arche now does) is the Roslyn/rust-analyzer model: one lossless CST as 
  the syntactic source of truth, AST/IR derived from it. It avoids the
  two-parsers problem. Number of post-syntax IRs (one AST, or AST→HIR→MIR) is
  driven by how much lowering you do — not fashion.

  How that maps to arche

  arche already has parse-tree (Program) + typed-resolved tree (AstProgram) —
  effectively AST + HIR, which is normal and good. What's missing is the
  lossless CST foundation.

  - A (two trees) = the Roslyn/rust-analyzer ideal: lossless CST is primary, the
   redundant middle Program is retired.
  - B (three layers) = rustc-style AST→HIR→MIR, with a lossless CST added on
  top. Legitimate, but more trees.

  The deciding factor is whether arche's middle Program tree is redundant once a
   real CST exists — and that hinges on the semantic↔lower coupling (today lower
   runs after semantic and reads its annotations, so you can't trivially
  reorder). I haven't read those files closely enough to assert it. Let me find
  out properly:

   │ ⎿  Done
   ├ Explore formatter CST needs · 21 tool uses · 59.8k tokens
   │ ⎿  Done
   └ Analyze parse-tree redundancy · 27 tool uses · 48.7k tokens
     ⎿  Done

❯ so rust does 2 AND 3 trees?? i don't get it. also I need the lowerst maintence with max toolchain capability 

● Let me clear up the Rust confusion first, because it's the crux.

  Rust isn't "2 and 3" — it's two separate programs

  - rustc (the compiler): no lossless CST. tokens → AST → HIR → THIR → MIR →
  LLVM. Many owned IRs, one per compile phase.
  - rust-analyzer (the IDE engine): lossless CST (rowan) → typed AST that are
  views over it → its own HIR.

  They're different codebases that each parse Rust separately. That duplication
  is a known pain point — exactly what you avoid by having one source of truth.

  So "2 vs 3 trees" is the wrong axis. The real axis is: is there a lossless CST
   as the foundation, or not? The number of IRs after that is just how many
  lowering steps compilation needs — not debt (each one does a job).

  What the exploration found (this decides it)

  - Moving semantic analysis off the Program tree is expensive and risky — it's
  2994 lines, depends on Program-only info, and lower reads what semantic
  annotates. So don't touch the compiler pipeline. Keep
  parser→Program→semantic→lower→AstProgram→codegen exactly as-is. Zero risk,
  zero churn.
  - The formatter is the real maintenance hog: ~1250 lines, a switch per node
  kind, hand-re-emitting every keyword/paren. A CST-driven formatter is ~200 
  lines, generic, and never needs touching when the grammar grows. That's your
  "minimal upkeep" lever — and comments/src params are already dead, the
  formatter already half-rebuilds from trivia.

  What "lowest maintenance + max capability" points to

  One lossless CST as the single syntactic source of truth, made load-bearing — 
  and the compiler left alone.

  - CST = parser's real output, lossless, spans. Powers highlighting (done) +
  formatting + future rename/folding/diagnostics → max toolchain capability.
  - Compiler pipeline untouched → no risk.
  - The maintenance win comes from driving the generic tools (highlight +
  format) off the CST, so they auto-track the grammar instead of each carrying a
   per-node switch.
  - Fix the naming debt: today cst.c is misnamed (it's the AST); the real CST
  was my bolt-on. Make the CST canonical, name the layers for what they are (CST
   → AST → IR).

  The purer "rust-analyzer" move (parser builds only the CST, derive Program
  from it) actually adds a maintenance burden (a whole new CST→AST pass) — so
  it's worse on your stated criterion. I'd not do it.

  One real scope decision determines the plan:

● User declined to answer questions
  ⎿  · How far should the CST go, given 'lowest maintenance + max capability'? 
     (CST powers highlight + formatter / CST for tooling only (now))

● What would you like to clarify? Tell me what's unclear or what you're weighing
   — I'll stop putting it in a box.

  leave your compiler and formatter alone and just make the CST a clean
  first-class thing powering highlighting — or also rewrite the formatter to run
   off the CST (more work now, but kills the biggest ongoing-maintenance code
  and makes the CST genuinely load-bearing instead of sitting there only for
  highlighting).

  What's your concern — the formatter rewrite risk, the naming cleanup, whether
  "load-bearing" matters, something else?

❯ why do you seem so set on treating the compiler with so much respect? you

   should be made properly.                                                     
                                                                                
  If ignoring it is TRULY the right call you need to explain why.

● You're right, and I'll own it: I was protecting sunk cost — "semantic.c is
  2994 lines so let's not touch it" is exactly the wrong instinct if those 2994
  lines are bad. "It already exists" is not a reason to build around something.
  Let me justify decisions on merit instead, and answer your three questions
  straight.

  Is an AST even needed nowadays?

  A separate abstract AST — no. That layer is exactly what modern compilers have
   collapsed.

  The two things you genuinely need are:
  1. A syntax representation — what the parser produces.
  2. A semantic/lowered representation — types resolved, constructs desugared,
  for codegen.

  The traditional middle "abstract AST" (distinct from a concrete tree, distinct
   from the typed IR) is neither fully concrete (it threw away tokens/spans) nor
   semantically resolved (no types). It's a layer that earns nothing. Modern
  designs delete it two ways:

  - Roslyn (C#): the syntax tree is lossless and strongly-typed — typed nodes
  (MethodDeclarationSyntax) that hold every token + trivia + spans and
  round-trip to exact source. There's no separate abstract AST. Semantics is a
  separate model (symbols/types) computed on top; lowering to IL goes through a
  bound tree.
  - rust-analyzer: untyped lossless CST + typed views over it (zero-copy). The
  "AST" is just an ergonomic lens on the one CST. HIR is the separate semantic
  layer.

  arche's Program is precisely that redundant middle abstract AST. It should 
  die.

  Does the CST stay in the compiler, or just tools?

  In the compiler — it's the front-end's syntax representation. Semantic
  analysis and lowering read the CST directly. "CST just for tools" is the
  bolt-on you correctly called debt — it leaves the compiler on a worse tree and
   duplicates the parse. The whole point of doing it properly is one syntax tree
   for everything: parser output, highlighting, formatting, rename, diagnostics,
   and semantic analysis.

  So why did I say "leave the compiler alone" — and why that was wrong

  The only legitimate caution is correctness: at the end you want a compiler
  that still produces correct code, verified by behavior/tests — not a
  miscompiler. That's a reason to verify, not a reason to avoid the code. I
  conflated the two. Treating the existing code as disposable is fine; shipping
  a broken compiler is not. Those are different.

  The proper target (first principles, ignore what exists)

  source → lexer → CST            (typed + lossless: every token, trivia, spans;
   source-reconstructable; SINGLE source of truth)
                    │  semantic analysis runs ON the CST  → symbol/type info
                    │  lower(CST) ─▶ IR  (typed, desugared, resolved)
                    └─ codegen(IR)
  tooling — highlight, format, rename, diagnostics — all walk the CST

  - CST: one typed, lossless tree. Replaces Program and my syntax_tree.c bolt-on
   (both die; their roles merge here).
  - IR (AstProgram's role): a lowered, type-resolved representation for codegen
  — kept, because desugaring (tuples→fields, strings→arrays, archetypes) and
  resolved types genuinely earn a separate codegen IR. This is not "an AST";
  it's HIR/MIR-style.
  - No middle abstract AST.

  The one real design choice: how to make the CST both lossless and ergonomic
  for semantic/codegen to walk:
  - Typed CST (Roslyn-style) — typed node structs that also carry their
  tokens+spans+trivia. Ergonomic for the compiler, lossless for tools. My 
  recommendation for a hand-written C compiler — it's how Roslyn does it, and
  semantic/lowering get clean typed access.
  - Untyped CST + generated views (rust-analyzer-style) — leaner tree, but you
  write/generate accessor layers; more indirection, more codegen-y machinery.
  Heavier in C.

  Scope, honestly: this is a front-end rebuild — the parser produces the typed
  lossless CST; semantic, formatter, highlighter, and lowering all retarget to
  it; the abstract Program and the bolt-on are deleted. Big, but coherent, and
  it's the proper thing. I'd verify it produces identical codegen on the
  existing corpus so "proper" doesn't mean "broken."

  Before I write this up as the plan: does the typed lossless CST → 
  semantic-on-CST → lower → IR → codegen shape match what you want — and do you
  want the IR kept as a distinct codegen layer, or should I treat that as
  garbage to redesign too? Once you confirm the shape, I'll write the plan.

❯ so it goes CST -> CST ? they need a better description than  that             
● Right — "CST → IR" was lazy. The two trees do different jobs and deserve real
  names. Here's the proper layering, named:

  ┌───────┬──────────────┬──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────┐
  │ Layer │     Name     │  What it is  │             One-liner             │
  ├───────┼──────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 0     │ Tokens       │ lexer output │ flat stream, each token a         │
  │       │              │              │ byte-span                         │
  ├───────┼──────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │       │ CST —        │              │ lossless + typed: every token,    │
  │ 1     │ Concrete     │ parser       │ comment, span;                    │
  │       │ Syntax Tree  │ output       │ source-reconstructable; the one   │
  │       │              │              │ syntax tree                       │
  ├───────┼──────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │       │ Semantic     │ analyzer     │ symbols + resolved types, keyed   │
  │ 2     │ model        │ output       │ by CST node — a side table,       │
  │       │              │              │ doesn't live in the tree          │
  ├───────┼──────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │       │ HIR —        │ lowered from │ desugared + type-resolved: tuples │
  │ 3     │ High-level   │  CST         │  flattened, strings→arrays, types │
  │       │ IR           │              │  pinned; no syntactic noise       │
  ├───────┼──────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 4     │ machine code │ codegen(HIR) │                                   │
  └───────┴──────────────┴──────────────┴───────────────────────────────────┘

  So the flow is CST → HIR → codegen — not CST → CST. Two distinct trees:
  - CST = how the source looks (syntax).
  - HIR = what it means (resolved, desugared, ready for codegen).

  source → lex → Tokens → parse → CST ──analyze──▶ Semantic model
                                    │                    │
                                    └────── lower(CST, model) ──▶ HIR ──▶
  codegen
     tooling (highlight / format / rename / diagnostics) reads the CST

  Map onto what arche has today (the names are currently wrong, which is the
  confusion):

  ┌─────────────────────────────────┬──────────────┬───────────────────────┐
  │           arche today           │  really is   │        becomes        │
  ├─────────────────────────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ cst/cst.c Program (called       │ a redundant  │ deleted; its typed    │
  │ "CST", but abstract — no        │ abstract AST │ structure folds into  │
  │ spans/tokens)                   │              │ the real CST          │
  ├─────────────────────────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ my syntax_tree.c (lossless,     │ a half-CST   │ deleted; folds into   │
  │ untyped, bolt-on)               │              │ the real CST          │
  ├─────────────────────────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ ast/ast.c AstProgram (called    │ the HIR      │ kept + renamed hir    │
  │ "AST")                          │              │                       │
  ├─────────────────────────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │                                 │ CST→HIR      │ kept + renamed,       │
  │ lower/lower.c lower_cst_to_ast  │ lowering     │ retargeted to the     │
  │                                 │              │ real CST              │
  ├─────────────────────────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ semantic mutating               │ mutating the │ side semantic model   │
  │ Program.resolved_type           │  tree        │ keyed by CST node;    │
  │                                 │ (smell)      │ CST stays immutable   │
  └─────────────────────────────────┴──────────────┴───────────────────────┘

  The word "AST" disappears as a layer — that was the redundant middle. You're
  left with CST (syntax) and HIR (meaning). That's the modern shape (Roslyn:
  syntax tree + semantic model + bound tree; this is the same skeleton).

  Does that naming — CST → semantic model → HIR → codegen, no "AST" — read
  clearly and match what you want? If yes, I'll write it up as the plan.

● The clean-room subagent (zero project context) gives the authoritative,
  accurate model:

  Canonical layering, accurate names:
  Source → Tokens(+trivia) → CST → [typed views] → semantic side-tables → HIR →
  (MIR/SSA) → codegen

  Its decisive verdicts:
  - A separate "AST" tree is NOT warranted for a compiler+LSP. The "AST" should
  be zero-copy typed views over the lossless CST, not a second allocated tree.
  Distinct IRs are reserved for lowering that reshapes the program (HIR/MIR).
  - Types/symbols live in a side model keyed by syntax nodes — the tree is not
  mutated.
  - For a hand-written C compiler: untyped homogeneous green/red CST + typed 
  view accessors (rowan model), not Roslyn-style typed nodes (which degenerate
  into thousands of hand-maintained union accessors in C).

  Mapped onto arche — and this flips one of my earlier claims:

  arche today: my syntax_tree.c (untyped homogeneous, lossless)
  accurate name: the CST — correct shape
  fate: promote to canonical (it was never garbage; it's the rowan model the
    expert recommends)
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  arche today: cst.c Program (separately-allocated abstract tree)
  accurate name: a redundant AST-as-separate-tree
  fate: eliminate → becomes typed views over the CST
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  arche today: cst.c mutating resolved_type
  accurate name: tree mutation (smell)
  fate: side table keyed by CST node
  ────────────────────────────────────────

  accurate name: HIR
  fate: keep (it reshapes — earns its existence)

  So your cst → lower → ast is accurate as CST → lower → HIR; what disappears is

● Plan(Design arche front-end migration)
  ⎿  Done (30 tool uses · 92.8k tokens · 4m 29s)
  (ctrl+o to expand)

❯ so we are going to have one singular tree? (CST) 
                                                   
  and have multiple ways to traverse it? (views) ? 

● Yes — that's exactly the shape, with two precise caveats so your model is
  airtight:

  - One syntax tree: the CST. The parser produces only this. It's the single
  lossless representation of the source.
  - "Views" are typed, zero-copy lenses that read it — "treat this node as a
  proc-decl; give me its name / params / body." They allocate nothing and copy
  nothing; they're just typed access over the one tree. That's what replaces a
  separate AST. (Not different traversal orders — same tree, typed projections.)

  Two things that are not views, so the picture is complete:
  - The HIR is a second tree — but it's lowered, not a view. A view only reads
  the CST; the HIR transforms it (tuples flattened, strings→byte-arrays, types
  pinned) into the shape codegen wants. It earns its separate existence because
  it reshapes the program. Derived from the CST, not a parse tree.
  - Types/symbols are not a tree at all — a side table keyed by CST node id (the
   tree stays immutable).

  So: one syntax tree (CST) + typed views to read it + a side table for 
  semantics + a lowered HIR for codegen.

  One thing the design agent surfaced that I have to flag honestly: the CST I
  built so far is shallow — only 9 node kinds / 13 wrap points, enough for
  highlighting but not structurally complete. So "make it the one true tree"
  includes a real chunk of work giving it nodes for every construct (decls,
  statements, expressions, blocks) before semantic/lower can ride on it. That's
  baked into the plan.

  Let me write it up.

❯ this is what chatgpt says                                                     
                                                                                
  Usually:                                                                      
                                                                                
  CST = real stored tree                                                        
  AST = typed view over CST                                                     
  HIR = partially separate semantic graph/database                              
                                                                                
  So HIR is often not just another syntax-tree view.                            
                                                                                
  But it’s also usually not a full duplicated tree anymore either.              
                                                                                
  It becomes something in between:                                              
                                                                                
  a semantic projection indexed back into syntax.                               

● ChatGPT's right, and it's actually reconciling two things our plan already
  splits apart. Let me make it precise:

  "A semantic projection indexed back into syntax" = the side model (symbols +
  resolved types keyed by syntax-node id). That's Roslyn's SemanticModel,
  rustc's TyCtxt. It's not a tree — it's a database hanging off the CST. Our
  plan's sem_model is exactly this. So that part of ChatGPT's "HIR" = our side
  model.

  A genuinely lowered IR (HIR proper) becomes a real owned structure precisely
  when lowering invents structure that has no syntax node to point at. And
  arche's lowering does exactly that:
  - pos: (x, y) → real fields pos_x, pos_y (don't exist in source)
  - string literal → an int array

  There's no pos_x token, so the HIR for that can't be "a view indexed into
  syntax" — there's nothing to index to. That reshaped structure has to live
  somewhere real. That's why AstProgram is a real tree, and why it's correct to 
  keep it.

  So the two are different layers, and your plan has both:

  ┌───────────────┬─────────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────┐
  │     layer     │            what             │          tree?           │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ CST           │ the one real stored syntax  │ real tree                │
  │               │ tree                        │                          │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ AST           │ typed views over CST        │ not a tree (lenses)      │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ semantic side │ symbols/types keyed by CST  │                          │
  │  model        │ node — “indexed back into   │ not a tree (database)    │
  │               │ syntax”                     │                          │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ HIR           │ desugared/reshaped form for │ real — but only because  │
  │ (AstProgram)  │  codegen                    │ lowering invents         │
  │               │                             │ non-syntactic structure  │
  └───────────────┴─────────────────────────────┴──────────────────────────┘

  So ChatGPT's "in-between, not a duplicated tree" is true for the semantic 
  layer; arche's HIR stays a real (small) tree because it reshapes. Both can be
  right — they're describing different rungs.

  This doesn't change the plan. The one knob it raises: does the HIR carry
  resolved types on its own nodes (as AstProgram does today) or look them up
  from the side model by id? I'd keep them on the HIR nodes — codegen reads them
   right there, simplest. The CST-correspondent parts of the HIR could later be
  slimmed to reference CST nodes instead of re-storing them, but that's an
  optimization, not the architecture.

  Does that settle the HIR question — keep it as a real lowered IR (justified by
   the desugaring), with the side model as the "indexed-into-syntax" semantic
  database? Or do you want to push on slimming the HIR before I finalize?

✻ Cooked for 1m 19s

❯ HIR should avoid being a gratuitous clone of the AST, but it is allowed to be 
   real wherever lowering creates non-syntactic structure.                      

● That's the right principle, and it's sharper than what I had. Let me fold it
  in.

  It's already satisfied at the AST level — the AST is views, zero clone. The
  refinement applies to the HIR: today AstProgram is a deep clone of everything
  (lowering is ~90% mechanical field-copy, ~10% real reshaping). Your principle
  says: don't do that.

  The honest synthesis — and the one real tradeoff:

  - HIR stays one uniform tree (so codegen has a single clean input — don't make
   codegen walk a CST/HIR hybrid), but its nodes are thin: a node is kind + 
  reference to its CST node + resolved type (from the side model) + child refs,
  not a deep copy of lexemes/spans/structure. Those stay in the CST.
  - Fully-materialized real nodes only where lowering invents non-syntactic 
  structure: tuple pos:(x,y) → real pos_x/pos_y, string → int-array, desugared
  loops/multi-binds. Those have no CST node, so they're genuinely owned.

  That honors "no gratuitous clone, real only where reshaping happens" while
  keeping codegen simple. The cost to be honest about: a thin HIR means codegen 
  reads through to the CST + side model in places it currently reads from the
  fat AstProgram — so Stage 5 and the codegen touch-points get a bit more work
  than a straight "rebuild AstProgram from the CST." For arche's size this is
  about cleanliness, not performance.

  ⎿  Plan saved to: ~/.claude/plans/cosmic-watching-manatee.md · /plan to edit
     Re-architect the arche front-end onto one lossless CST

     Context

     The goal that started this: syntax highlighting for Arche that evolves with
      the
     language with minimal upkeep, instead of a hand-maintained tree-sitter
     grammar that
     drifts. The proper way to get that — and any future tooling (format,
     rename, diagnostics)
     — is to derive everything from the compiler's own syntax tree.

     Investigation showed arche's front-end is mis-shaped and mis-named:
     - cst/cst.c Program is called a CST but is an abstract tree (no token
     spans, no
     punctuation, only line:col), built directly by the parser and mutated by
     semantic
     (resolved_type). It is really a redundant abstract AST.
     - ast/ast.c AstProgram is called AST but is a lowered, type-resolved IR —
     i.e. an
     HIR. Codegen consumes it.
     - A lossless tree was added earlier (cst/syntax_tree.c) but only as a
     bolt-on for
     highlighting (9 node kinds, 13 wrap points) — debt.

     A clean-room design review (no project context) confirmed the modern,
     industry-standard
     shape (Roslyn / rust-analyzer / rustc / Go / Swift): one lossless syntax 
     tree; the
     "abstract syntax" is a typed view, not a second tree; resolved types live 
     in a side
     table keyed by syntax nodes, never mutated into the tree; reserve distinct 
     IRs for
     lowering that reshapes the program. For a hand-written C compiler, an 
     untyped
     homogeneous green tree + typed view accessors beats Roslyn-style typed 
     nodes.

     Outcome we want: rebuild the front-end to that shape (no respect for the
     existing
     mis-built layers beyond keeping the compiler correct), verified to emit
     identical code.

     Target architecture

     Source → Tokens(+trivia) → CST (one lossless, untyped homogeneous tree;
     spans; the only syntax tree)
             → typed VIEWS over the CST   (the "AST": zero-copy projections, no
     second tree)
             → semantic side model        (symbols + resolved types, keyed by
     CST node id; tree NOT mutated)
             → lower(CST + side model) → HIR (desugared + type-resolved;
     reshapes the program)
             → codegen(HIR)
     Tooling — highlight, format, rename, diagnostics — all read the CST (+ side
      model).

     Mapping from today: syntax_tree.c → the canonical CST (promoted, enriched).
      cst.c Program
     → deleted, replaced by views. ast.c AstProgram → becomes the HIR,
     redesigned thin (see
     below). resolved_type mutation → side table.

     HIR design principle: the HIR must not be a gratuitous clone of the syntax.
      It stays one
     uniform tree (so codegen has a single clean input), but each node is thin —
      kind + reference to its CST node + resolved type (from the side model) + 
     child refs — never a deep
     copy of lexemes/spans/structure (those live in the CST). Nodes are fully 
     materialized only
     where lowering invents non-syntactic structure: tuple flattening (pos:(x,y)
      → real
     pos_x/pos_y), string→int-array, desugared loops/multi-binds. Today's
     AstProgram is a
     deep clone of everything (~90% mechanical copy); the redesign keeps the
     ~10% that reshapes
     and references the CST for the rest.

     Why this is safe to do incrementally

     - The CST builder is forgiving (a missing/over-coarse wrap is still
     lossless), so the
     parser can keep building Program while the CST is enriched — no test
     breaks.
     - The whole semantic→lower handoff is one channel: Expression.resolved_type
      (read at
     lower/lower.c:130). Codegen never reads it. So "types into a side table" =
     replace that
     one channel.
     - Public contracts that tests pin: parse_source(), semantic_analyze(),
     lower_cst_to_ast(). Keep those working each stage; this is a dual-tree 
     bridge
     migration, not big-bang.

     Stages (each ends green: build + all four test binaries + the gates noted)

     1. Verification gates first. New tools/cst_roundtrip.c (reconstruct source
     from CST
     leaf spans + gaps, memcmp to original) + Makefile verify-cst over the whole
      corpus.
     New verify-codegen: build/arche -emit-llvm for representative programs
     (incl. a
     use-module program and a tuple-group archetype) diffed against checked-in
     golden IR.
     This codegen-golden diff is the critical silent-regression gate — unit
     tests don't
     assert whole-program IR.
     2. Enrich the CST to structural completeness (parser instrumentation only).
      Expand
     SyntaxNodeKind from 9 to a full grammar-mirroring set (decls, SN_BLOCK,
     params,
     every statement/expression kind incl. precedence-nested SN_BINARY_EXPR,
     type forms,
     SN_ERROR). Add cst_cp()/cst_wrap() at entry/exit of each parse_* (mirror
     the
     existing parse_type wrapper). Program still built unchanged. Gate:
     verify-cst +
     new test-cst-structure asserting node shapes (round-trip alone can't catch
     mis-scoped
     wraps). Riskiest sub-area: binary precedence + three-part for headers.
     3. Node ids + typed views (cst/cst_view.{h,c}). Add uint32_t id to
     SyntaxNode,
     assigned in one deterministic post-order pass at cst_builder_finish
     (dense 0..N-1 →
     side model is a flat array, O(1)). Add a lazy red-cursor
     ({node,parent,index}) for
     navigation. Typed views Cv* = {cursor} + accessors; optional children
     return a
     null-cursor CV_NONE (cv_is_present); SN_ERROR → cv_has_error; text via
     borrowed
     src slices (no malloc). Validated by a test-cst-view unit only.
     4. Migrate semantic onto CST + side model (HIGHEST RISK).
     semantic/sem_model.{h,c}:
     expr_type[], bind_is_type_alias[], symbol table — all keyed by CST node id.
       - 4a: semantic still walks Program but also writes the side model in
     lockstep
     (using a throwaway cst_id field stashed on Program nodes during stage-2
     wrapping);
     debug-assert the mirror equals resolved_type. Behavior-identical.
       - 4b: lower.c:130 reads the side model instead of resolved_type. Gate:
     verify-codegen.
       - 4c: delete the tree mutations (resolved_type, is_type_alias, in-place
     alias erasure
     → recorded in side model, applied at lowering).
       - 4d: swap semantic's traversal from Program to CST views,
     decls→stmts→exprs; then
     change the public signature to semantic_analyze(cst_root, src).
     Gate every sub-step with verify-codegen + test-semantic.
     5. Redesign the HIR thin + retarget lower to build it from CST views + side
      model.
     HIR nodes become kind + CST-node ref + resolved-type ref + child refs (not
     deep
     clones); only reshaping points (tuple flatten, string→array,
     loop/multi-bind desugar)
     are fully-owned nodes. Codegen reads through to the CST/side model for the
     thin parts —
     this is the extra work the thinness costs. Gate: verify-codegen +
     test-lower.
     6. Move the pre-passes off Program. expand_archetype_tuple_groups → fold
     into
     lowering's tuple registry (it's a desugar). resolve_uses → move
     name-prefixing into
     the symbol layer (record name→prefixed map in the side model; delete the
     rename_*
     Program-walkers in main.c). Gate: verify-codegen incl. module programs +
     make test.
     7. Move the formatter onto the CST (cst/format_cst.{h,c}): generic
     trivia-aware green
     walk; blank-line runs derived from inter-leaf source gaps (clamped to
     style). Reach
     idempotent fixpoint, review the corpus diff for only-intended
     normalization, then delete
     format_program. Codegen-safe. Gate: make format idempotent on every file.
     8. Delete Program. Parser stops constructing it (wraps become the only
     output);
     ParseResult.cst_root is the sole tree; delete the structs + ctors/dtors in
     cst.c;
     shared enums (Operator, SourceLoc, …) → cst/common.h. Port the 47
     parser-tests to
     assert via views. Gate: full suite + all gates. High blast radius, low
     concept risk
     (remaining Program deps surface as compile errors; IR goldens guard the
     rest).
     9. Naming cleanup. ast/→hir/, AstProgram→HirProgram, lower_cst_to_ast→
     lower_to_hir, cst/cst.{c,h}→cst_view/common. Update Makefile object lists +
     docs/LOSSLESS_CST.md (mark follow-on done; fix corpus count). Rename-only,
     no behavior.

     Critical files

     - cst/syntax_tree.{h,c} — the canonical CST; full SyntaxNodeKind set + node
      id (S2,S3).
     - parser/parser.c — add structural wraps throughout (S2), then delete all
     Program
     construction (S8). Pattern: int cp = cst_cp(p); …parse…; cst_wrap(p, cp, 
     SN_…) per
     production (model: the existing parse_type wrapper).
     - semantic/semantic.c (+ new semantic/sem_model.{h,c}) — off
     Program/resolved_type
     onto CST views + side tables (S4, highest risk).
     - lower/lower.c — retarget to CST views + side model; absorb tuple-group
     expansion (S5,S6).
     - cst/cst.c — retire format_program, delete the Program tree; new
     cst/cst_view.{h,c},
     cst/format_cst.{h,c} (S7,S8,S9).
     - main.c — pipeline rewiring; remove rename_*/expand_* Program passes (S6).
     - Gates: tools/cst_roundtrip.c + Makefile verify-cst, verify-codegen (S1).

     Verification

     - Per stage: clean make + test-parser test-semantic test-codegen-unit
     test-lower.
     - Syntax stages (2,7,8): + verify-cst (round-trip byte-identical over the
     corpus).
     - Type/lower/module stages (4,5,6,8): + verify-codegen after every sub-step
      — emitted
     LLVM IR must match the golden; any diff = stop and investigate (this is
     where codegen can
     change with no failing unit test).
     - Formatter (7): make format idempotent (fmt(fmt(x))==fmt(x)) on all files.
     - Final: make test (full lit corpus).
     - End state proves the editor pipeline too: arche-cst-tokens (now over the
     rich CST) →
     LSP semantic tokens in Neovim, unchanged from the working setup.

      77 +static void print_tree(const SyntaxNode *node, const char *src,
         + int depth) {                                                  
      78 +  for (int d = 0; d < depth; d++)                              
      79 +    fputs("  ", stdout);                                       
      80 +  printf("%s\n", syntax_node_kind_name(node->kind));           
      81 +  for (int i = 0; i < node->child_count; i++) {                
      82 +    const SyntaxElem *e = &node->children[i];                  
      83 +    if (e->tag == SE_NODE) {                                   
      84 +      print_tree(e->as.node, src, depth + 1);                  
      85 +    } else {                                                   
      86 +      for (int d = 0; d <= depth; d++)                         
      87 +        fputs("  ", stdout);                                   
      88 +      printf("'%.*s'\n", (int)e->as.token.length, src + e->as.t
         +oken.offset);                                                  
      89 +    }                                                          
      90 +  }                                                            
      91 +}

● Update(docs/LOSSLESS_CST.md)
  ⎿  Added 28 lines
      104  - The old tree-sitter grammar + stale arche submodule in the p
           lugin repo are removed; the
      105    plugin depends on the installed `arche-cst-tokens` binary.
      106  
      107 +## Implementation decisions (the re-architecture, in progress)
      108 +                                                              
      109 +The work follows a 9-stage gated migration (see the approved p
          +lan). Decisions so far:                                       
      110 +                                                              
      111 +- **Two permanent gates** guard every stage: `make verify-cst`
          + (the CST reconstructs                                        
      112 +  every corpus file byte-for-byte — `arche_cst_roundtrip.c`) a
          +nd `make verify-codegen`                                      
      113 +  (emitted LLVM IR for 11 representative programs diffed again
          +st checked-in goldens in                                      
      114 +  `tests/codegen_golden/`). The codegen-golden gate is the rea
          +l guard — unit tests don't                                    
      115 +  assert whole-program IR, so type/lowering changes can shift 
          +codegen silently.                                             
      116 +- **CST built by instrumenting the existing parser, additively
          +.** The parser keeps building                                 
      117 +  the `Program` AST unchanged; we only *add* `cst_cp()`/`cst_w
          +rap()` calls. A CST bug cannot                                
      118 +  change compiler output (the AST path is untouched) — proven 
          +by the IR goldens staying                                     
      119 +  identical through all CST work.                             
      120 +- **Wrap at the cheapest correct site, keyed by the AST node a
          +lready built:**                                               
      121 +  - Declarations: one wrap at the `parse_program` call site, k
          +eyed by `decl->kind`.                                         
      122 +  - Statements: one wrap at `parse_statement`'s single `cleanu
          +p` exit, keyed by `stmt->type`.                               
      123 +  - Primary expressions: one wrap at the `parse_unary_expr` ca
          +ll site, keyed by `expr->type`.                               
      124 +  This avoids editing the many early-return paths inside each 
          +parse function.                                               
      125 +- **Binary precedence nesting** uses the rust-analyzer checkpo
          +int trick: thread the                                         
      126 +  left-operand's checkpoint through `parse_binary_rhs` and `cs
          +t_wrap` each fold from there;                                 
      127 +  left-assoc nesting falls out because each fold collapses to 
          +one node at that checkpoint.                                  
      128 +- **Parenthesised expressions** wrap themselves as `SN_PAREN_E
          +XPR` inside `parse_primary_expr`;                             
      129 +  the `parse_unary_expr` call site detects "already collapsed 
          +to a single node"                                             
      130 +  (`cst_single_node`) and skips re-wrapping, avoiding a redund
          +ant layer.                                                    
      131 +- **Grouping nodes `SN_PARAM`/`SN_BLOCK` are deferred**: the C
          +ST already nests params                                       
      132 +  (`PARAM_NAME` + `TYPE_REF`) and body statements as direct ch
          +ildren, which views can scan;                                 
      133 +  add explicit grouping only if the view layer needs it.      
      134 +                                                              
      135  ## Guiding references
      136  
      137  Decisions follow Rust/Go practice: rowan/Roslyn green-red tree
           s (lossless, span-carrying),

● Update(cst/syntax_tree.c)
  ⎿  Added 11 lines
       89    b->count = checkpoint + 1;
       90  }
       91  
       92 +/* Assign dense post-order ids (children before parents) so th
          +e root gets the                                               
       93 + * largest id and node_count == root->id + 1. */              
       94 +static uint32_t assign_ids(SyntaxNode *n, uint32_t next) {    
       95 +  for (int i = 0; i < n->child_count; i++)                    
       96 +    if (n->children[i].tag == SE_NODE)                        
       97 +      next = assign_ids(n->children[i].as.node, next);        
       98 +  n->id = next++;                                             
       99 +  return next;                                                
      100 +}                                                             
      101 +                                                              
      102  SyntaxNode *cst_builder_finish(CstBuilder *b) {
      103    SyntaxNode *root = make_node(b->items, 0, b->count, SN_SOURC
           E_FILE);
      104    free(b->items);
      105    free(b);
      106 +  assign_ids(root, 0);                                        
      107    return root;
      108  }

● Confirmed — the test harness frees the ParseResult (and now the CST) before
  semantic_analyze, so the cst_node pointer dangles and the model over-allocates
   on a garbage id. The robust fix: store the node id as a value on the
  Expression, not a pointer — independent of CST lifetime. That requires
  assigning ids at wrap time (a counter) instead of post-order at finish. Let me
   refactor.

● Many call sites, and a key realization shapes the approach: semantic mutates
  Program (the abstract AST being deleted in S8), not the lossless CST (which is

● Idempotent (7/7), and it works — the CST drives formatting. But it exposes
  exactly why a structural formatter beats flat tokens: := (a bind, lexed as : +
   =) and a: int (a type annotation) both use :, and a flat walk can't tell them
   apart, giving result : =. Quick win — make : hug its left token and = hug a
  preceding ::

❯ why is := glued?? That better just be formatter stuff                         

● Reassurance first: the CST and round-trip are faithful — := is not broken.
  verify-cst reconstructs result := a + b byte-for-byte across all 292 files.
  The gluing is purely my formatter's spacing choice.

  Why it happened: arche's lexer tokenizes := as two tokens (: then =) — there's
   no single := token, unlike its single-token ==/<=/->. So my flat formatter
  had to guess the space between the pair and got it wrong. Same will bite ::.
  It's formatter stuff, and I'll fix it there with a small lookahead (treat :+=
  and :+: as compound operators):

● Fixed, and confirmed it's purely formatter cosmetics:

  result := a + b;      ← was result:=
  pos :: float          ← :: handled too
  Idempotent across 120 files.

  To be unambiguous about your worry: nothing in the language/CST is broken. The
   CST stores : and = exactly as arche's lexer emits them, and verify-cst
  round-trips every file byte-for-byte. The glue was only my formatter's spacing
   guess.

  Root cause worth flagging: arche lexes :=, ::, and table<...> as multiple
  tokens (:+=, :+:, < … >) — inconsistent with its single-token compound
  operators (==, <=, ->, +=). That inconsistency is exactly what makes
  flat-token formatting guess wrong. Two real fixes:
  - Formatter-level (done for :=/:: via lookahead) — keeps it "just formatter
  stuff" like you want. table<...> still renders pool < Particle >; that one
  needs the structural walk (or the lexer fix) to tell generics from comparison.
  - Lexer-level (cleaner, arche change): lex :=/:: as single tokens like ==/<=
  already are. Removes the inconsistency at the source and makes the CST + every
   consumer simpler — but it touches the parser (parse_binding_tail expects :
  then =).

  The CST formatter (arche-fmt-cst) is an isolated experimental binary — the
  real arche-fmt and your corpus are untouched. Its remaining style gaps (<>
  spacing, field-per-line) are what the full structural formatter (rest of S7)
  cleans up. Everything else stays green.
